♠ Original name ♣ Egypt - Saite Period ♥ [1]
♠ Alternative names ♣ 26th Dynasty; Manethonian Dynasty; Saite Renaissance; Saite Dynasty ♥ 26th Dynasty; Manethonian Dynasty; Saite Renaissance [2] Saite Dynasty [3]
♠ Peak Date ♣ 550 BCE ♥ By the rule of Amasis (Ahmose II) Upper Egypt was secured, monarchy centralised, administration and finance administration was developed. Revenues able to support building of a fleet to pursue Mediterranean policy and conquer Cyprus. However, this came at a hefty financial cost. [4] Herodotus (II, 177, 1): "It is said that it was during the reign of Ahmose II that Egypt attained its highest level of prosperity both in respect of what the river gave the land and in respect of what the land yielded to men and that the number of inhabited cities at that time reached in total 20,000."
♠ Duration ♣ 664-525 BCE ♥ Saite Dynasty: 664-525 BCE. [5]
♠ Degree of centralization ♣ loose; unitary state ♥ Loose during the period Thebaid had most independence then unitary state.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Saite kings centralized the state considerably compared to preceding period. [6]
♠ Supra-polity relations ♣ none ♥

♠ relationship to preceding (quasi)polity ♣ replacement ♥ None of "continuity, cultural assimilation, elite migration, population migration" apply because the preceding Neo-Assyrian Empire was a foreign dynasty.
♠ succeeding (quasi)polity ♣ Achaemenid Empire ♥

♠ Language ♣ Demotic ♥ Demotic was introduced in the early Saite period, and spread throughout Egypt. A very important phenomenon.♠ Linguistic family ♣ Afro-Asiatic ♥ The Egyptian language is in the Afro-Asiatic language family.
♠ Religion Genus ♣ Egyptian Religions ♥ In the last millennium CE, a number of change occurred in Egyptian religion. They include: 1. A renewed "quest for new religious solutions which emphasized individual salvation. Generally, people were perhaps less optimistic that a virtuous life would bring them a blessed eternity, and more reliance was placed on the gods as a source of comfort. Traditional preparations for the afterlife continued, but people were also determined to enjoy their daily pleasures while they were still alive." [8] 2. "Although weak and foreign dynasties tried to revive the myth of the god-king in order to support their royal status, the political power of the king continued to decline, along with his influence on religion." [9] 3. An "increased association between the temples and lay people. One noticeable feature was an increase in the custom of setting up personal statues inside the temples so that the owners could benefit from the regular rituals for the gods (which were the same as those performed in earlier periods), and thus receive sustenance from the divine food offerings. It also emphasized the owner's devotion to the god, and gave him a claim on the deity's attention. This tradition emphasized the fact that the gods were now regarded as better guarantors of individual postmortem protection than the age-old mortuary cults." "One of the most characteristic developments of this later period is the importance of animal cults." [10] 4. "One of the most characteristic developments of this later period is the importance of animal cults." [11] Because it doesn't seem that these changes affected most of the variables we are interested in, it seems reasonable to infer continuity with earlier periods in terms of equality, prosociality, slavery, human sacrifice, and morality. Indeed, the the first millennium BCE was characterised by a "deep-rooted revival of things past that affected many aspects of Egypt's court culture, religion, script, literature, art, architecture, and burial practices." [12]

♠ Alternate Religion Genus ♣ ♥
♠ Alternate Religion Family ♣ ♥
♠ Alternate Religion ♣ ♥If scholars are in disagreement over whether Egypt of the Kushite Empire experienced a period of centralized rule, this undoubtedly did occur during the Twenty-sixth Dynasty (664-525 BCE),[13] which has been called the 'Saite Renaissance'. The Saite kings, from their palace at Memphis,[14] gradually managed to construct a considerably more centralized state than that of the preceding period.[15] The high point of Saite power was the reign of Amasis II, whose administration commanded sufficient revenues to enable him to build a fleet to conquer Cyprus.[16] An important phenomenon of this period was the development of the Demotic script (from the Greek word demos, 'the people'), which originated in the delta but spread throughout Lower and Upper Egypt under Psamtik I and his successors.[17] Based on Hieratic, a cursive script using simplified Egyptian hieroglyphics, Demotic was useful in everyday contexts such as accounting, letter-writing and non-religious literature, which grew in importance during the period.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Population and political organization[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Upper Egypt, which before the Kushites had long been ruled by a militarized priesthood, was always a difficult nut for the central administration in the Nile Delta to crack. At first, the main influence the Saites had over the Twenty-fifth Dynasty aristocracy at Thebes was through the priestly position of the God's Wife of Amun. This was held by a Saite priestess at the Amun temple, and an observer (rsw) based in Thebes who was often called 'governor'.[18][19] Established in the Kushite Period, the God's Wife of Amun role was of 'very great and publicly acknowledged' political importance.[20][MAJIDBENAM_BR]To increase their hold over Egypt, Saite strategy, under the first ruler Psamtik I, sought the removal of the nome system of administration; instead a military official was directly appointed to oversee the whole Southern Land (covering the region from Aswan to Memphis).[21] Named leader of the fleet and based in Herakleopolis, this official also acted as the 'revenue accountant for Middle and Upper Egypt'.[22] The Saite king ruled from Memphis, where there was a High Council of aristocrats who reported directly to him.[23] The vizier acted as the supreme judge of the realm.[24] In the Late Period, almost all officials were also priests[25] and oracles featured in the elections of officials and even of kings.[26] The military was usually led by the supreme chief of the expedition,[27] but under Amasis II the chief physician also occupied important military roles such as 'leader of the Aegean foreign (troops)' and 'admiral of the royal fleet'.[28][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Theban Egypt was not brought under the effective control of the Memphite kings until Psamtik II's campaign against the Kingdom of Napata between 592 and 591, which ended what had essentially been a 'period of compromise' in Upper Egypt.[29] Before that time, Saite authority was so weak that they were forced to acknowledge the legitimacy of the Kushite pharaohs of the Twenty-fifth Dynasty. After Psamtik II's military successes of the 590s, however, their policies changed and they began to portray the Nubian kings as usurpers.[30] With the Saite Dynasty now in a more powerful position, there was a return to the nome system of provincial organization, with governors once again stationed in the regions.[31] The title of leader of the fleet seems to disappear around this time,[32] and by 592 BCE Herakleopolis had a 'governor'.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Unfortunately, again, reliable population estimates at this time are difficult to find, but every indication suggests that the total population remained fairly steady at roughly three million people.[33]

Hierarchical Complexity[edit]

♠ Settlement hierarchy ♣ [4-5] ♥ levels. At most: (1) Capital; (2) Regional centres; (3) Minor centres; (4) Villages; (5) Hamlets. Inferred from previous periods.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]1. Capital Memphis.[MAJIDBENAM_BR] Palace[MAJIDBENAM_BR]2. City eg. Mendes. "During the Late Period, provincial centers display much diversity and prosperity. Mendes, a city sacred to the ram god Banebdjed, contains a series of massive temple enclosures, a ram hypogeum, an elaborate shrine dedicated to Shu, Geb, Osiris, and Re, shrines built by Nectanebo I-II, private and royal burials (e.g., Nepherites), and other structures (Hansen 1999: 497; Redford and Redford 2005: 170-94). Explor- ation outside the temple precincts at Mendes suggests the residential area lay to the east and south, with a harbor to the east (Redford 2005: 8). In addition, geophysical surveys have been used at Buto and Tell el-Balamun to reveal much of the Saite settlement (Herbich and Hartung 2004: 16; Herbich and Spencer 2006: 17)." [45]

"In sum, the first Saite Period, the seventh century, was a reign of skillful politics aimed at taking over the territory. The second period, the sixth century (the years 592-591 could well mark this turning point), was an age of administrative standardization, and P. Rylands 9 reveals a country divided into nomes (ts.w)" e.g. Oxyrhynchos, Hermopolis, Cynopolis. "even if the territorial powers of intermediate level between the nome and the city, like the 'district-q'h.t', continue to be problematic."[80][MAJIDBENAM_BR]A "superintendent of the central treasury" and a "chief steward" are known under Apries and Amasis.[81]

Professions[edit]

♠ Occupational complexity ♣ ♥
♠ Professional military officers ♣ present ♥
♠ Professional soldiers ♣ present ♥ Infantrymen "received specific training during maneuvers held in camps ... very probably under the supervision of instructor officers like the directors of young army recruits." [108] Mercenaries, "notably from the eastern part of the Greek world, because of their technological sophistication, including their use of elaborate bronze armor and new military tactics."[109][MAJIDBENAM_BR]In the "Late period ... part of the army, perhaps 10 percent, was also made up of Egyptian professional soldiers. ... under the Saite dynasty Egyptian troops fought sometimes on the same side as foreign mercenaries, at other times against them. As to the garrisons, rulers did not rely only on mercenaries but also on Egyptian soldiers, for example in Elephantine, where mercenaries speaking Semetic languages later joined them." [110]
♠ Professional priesthood ♣ present ♥ [111]

Bureaucracy characteristics[edit]

♠ Full-time bureaucrats ♣ present ♥ From about 591 BCE: "specialized departments composed of financial, tax, logistics, and other specialists assigned to monitor local administrations".[112] Before this time the bureaucracy less specialised and titles of powerful individuals did not adequately (for historians) express their roles. e.g. Leader of the Fleet.[113] However these generalists were full-time? or had scribes who were full-time.
♠ Source of support ♣ suspected unknown ♥ previous code: governed population; land (the explanation below does not support these codes; unclear what these are based on)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Trade revenues very important. Incense route. Customs administration title: "Agent at the gate of foreign countries." Trade caravans could have military protection. [114]
♠ Examination system ♣ inferred absent ♥ Not encountered any reference to an examination system.
♠ Merit promotion ♣ inferred present: 664-571 BCE; inferred absent: 570-525 BCE ♥ In 570 BCE Apries was "swept from the throne by a machimoi backlash against the privileged position of Greeks and Carians in the military establishment."[115]
♠ Specialized government buildings ♣ inferred present ♥ Accounting office for royal domain. [116][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"Instructions of 'nh-Ssnky (C.III.1)" refers to a prison.[117] However not all scholars date text to Saite Period.[118]

Law[edit]

♠ Formal legal code ♣ present ♥ Caroline Arlte (Ed: need to check spelling of surname) book on Egyptian code [119]
♠ Judges ♣ inferred absent ♥ inferred absent or unknown. In temples. Were these judges priests? If so will need to code absent because we are coding judges as a specialized position.
♠ Courts ♣ inferred absent ♥ inferred absent or unknown. In temples. there was a kind of court process but there may not have been a "court building" [120][MAJIDBENAM_BR]In Late Period Egypt "Egyptian women (unlike Greeks) could act in transactions on their own behalf and without any guardian whatsoever; equally, women could come forward in law-courts totally unaided as plaintiffs or defendants. And it is quite evident that women were capable of independent economic activities regardless of marital status."[121]
♠ Professional Lawyers ♣ inferred absent ♥ There were judges in temples. However, one must infer that even if these judges were not also priests (which is unknown?) that due to an apparent lack of specialized court-infrastructure professional lawyers would be very unlikely.

Specialized Buildings: polity owned[edit]

♠ Special purpose house ♣ present ♥
♠ Utilitarian public buildings ♣ present ♥
♠ irrigation systems ♣ present ♥
♠ drinking water supply systems ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ markets ♣ present ♥ "The cities that were members of the Hellenion received the right to manage the only authorised trade zone connecting Egypt with the Mediterranean world. They appointed the "provosts" of this port market, the prostatai tou emporiou, according to Herodotus (II.178-179). The pharaohs nevertheless maintained within this area a royal establishment in charge of collecting taxes levied in the port." [122]
♠ food storage sites ♣ present ♥ Granaries. [123] Although rare in Late Period texts, the term pr-nswt "seems to be perceived as an architectural entity comprehending the treasury and storage facilities".[124] New Kingdom text "The Duties of the Vizier" (TT 100) "refer to the pr-nswt as the centre of royal government where two important dignities of the administration, the vizier and the treasurer, performed their functions: controlling the incomes and outcomes of this institution, guaranteeing the security and justice as well as inspecting the personnel of the palace or organizing the army within it. Another role of the pr-nswt consisted in receiving reports from Egyptian provinces to update the government on happenings in outlying areas of the state. ...... Several other compositions refer to the pr-nswt as place where entries and outflows were recorded and physically stored."[125]
♠ Symbolic buildings ♣ present ♥Sanctuaries and temples.[126][MAJIDBENAM_BR] Temple of Seth in Mut el-Kharab (Psamtik I - Psamtik II)[MAJIDBENAM_BR] Oasis of Kharga - perhaps Temple of Hibis dates to reign of Psamtik II

♠ Canals ♣ present ♥ Canal dug linking Nile to the Red Sea during the reign of Neckau II.[128][129]

Information[edit]

♠ Length ♣ inferred present ♥ A measurement system was standardized in the New Kingdom. Can infer they still had this or a derivative.
♠ Area ♣ inferred present ♥ A measurement system was standardized in the New Kingdom. Can infer they still had this or a derivative.
♠ Volume ♣ inferred present ♥ A measurement system was standardized in the New Kingdom. Can infer they still had this or a derivative.
♠ Weight ♣ inferred present ♥ A measurement system was standardized in the New Kingdom. Can infer they still had this or a derivative.
♠ Time ♣ inferred present ♥ Ancient Egyptians had sundials (gnomon) from 3500 BCE. By 1500 BCE Egyptian sundials are known to have split daytime into 12 parts. An Egyptian water clock was found in the tomb of Amenhotep I, dating to c.1500 BCE. The Egyptians used merkhets to calculate time at night from the position of the stars.
♠ Geometrical ♣ inferred present ♥ Knowledge passed down from heritage.

♠ Written records ♣ present ♥ "standardization of all private documents pertaining to family income" implied by Heredotus's claim that Saites (under Amasis) taxed household income and assets. At this very time demotic Egyptian replaced abnormal hieratic at Thebes.[131]
♠ Script ♣ present ♥ Abnormal hieratic. Demotic. Theban cursive writing. [132] Theban cursive and Abnormal Hieratic are the same; replaced by Demotic. See e.g. K. Donker Van Heel, The lost battle of Peteamonip son of Petehorresne. In Acta Demotica, Acts of the Fifth International Conference for Demotists, Pisa, 4th-8th September 1993, ed. E. Bresciani, 115-24. Egitto e Vicino Oriente 17. [133] "standardization of all private documents pertaining to family income" implied by Heredotus's claim that Saites (under Amasis) taxed household income and assets. At this very time demotic Egyptian replaced abnormal hieratic at Thebes.[134]
♠ Non-phonetic writing ♣ present ♥ hieroglyphs
♠ Phonetic alphabetic writing ♣ absent ♥ The Coptic alphabet is the script used for writing the Coptic language. The repertoire of glyphs is based on the Greek alphabet augmented by letters borrowed from the Egyptian Demotic and is the first alphabetic script used for the Egyptian language.[135]

Military Technologies[edit]

♠ Iron ♣ present ♥ "iron armor and weapons of a non-Egyptian type found in Egypt attest the work of Greek blacksmiths specialized in making and fixing weapons in the military settlements of Daphnae and Migdol.[154] Even by the time the Achaemenid Empire conquered Egypt in 525 BCE, Egyptian relied on copper-alloy weapons. [155] However, the first iron weapons may well have been used much earlier and by foreign mercenaries during the Saite Period.

Vulnerable to counter-attacks, slinger units were usually small and used at the start of the battle. Because of the training required to produce and effective slinger they were often hired mercenaries.[157] Saite Kingdom used Greek mercenaries. In this period there were highly-trained slingers in the Mediterranean region (e.g. Balearic slingers). It is probable that the Saites also employed slingers.

Military Organization[edit]

♠ Standing armies ♣ present ♥ Troops had permanent bases. [206] In the "Late period ... part of the army, perhaps 10 percent, was also made up of Egyptian professional soldiers. ... under the Saite dynasty Egyptian troops fought sometimes on the same side as foreign mercenaries, at other times against them. As to the garrisons, rulers did not rely only on mercenaries but also on Egyptian soldiers, for example in Elephantine, where mercenaries speaking Semetic languages later joined them." [207]
♠ Uniformity of equipment ♣ inferred absent ♥ Greek infantry equipment excavated at Tell Defenna.[208]
♠ Total army ♣ 30,000: 570 BCE ♥ "There may have been 30,000 Greek mercenaries (epikouroi) around 570 BC (Herodotus 2.161-3; Diodorus 1.68.1)" [209] However, there were also Egyptian soldiers; they revolted in 570 BCE.
♠ Professional army ♣ 30,000: 570 BCE ♥ "There may have been 30,000 Greek mercenaries (epikouroi) around 570 BC (Herodotus 2.161-3; Diodorus 1.68.1)" [210] However, there were also Egyptian soldiers; it was they who revolted in 570 BCE.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]"The size of the Greek mercenary garrison is also unknown: Herodotus talks of 30,000 Greek and Carian mercenaries coming to the defence of Apries in his battle with Amasis, a figure probably without a firm foundation but meant to indicate an order of magnitude."[211]
♠ Largest fielded army ♣ ♥
♠ Maximum logistical lag ♣ ♥ An ox cart could travel 2 mph for five hours. Five horses could carry the same load at 4 mph for 8 hours.[212] That would be 16 km per day for an ox cart. 51 km per day for a five-team horse-cart. Assyrians introduced the five-team horse-cart. Egyptians may not have had enough horses to do this.
♠ Sources of recruits ♣ mercenaries; foreign ♥ "Foreign legions" from the East and the Aegean.[213] Ionians and Carians, perhaps from Psamtik II. [214] Cypriots, Judeans, Phoenicians. [216][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Greeks, Carians, Phoenicians, Cypriotes, Aramaeans, Jews.[217][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Mercenaries: "Our documentation lays much emphasis on those of Greek and Carian extraction, but we also hear of Jews, Phoenicians, and possibly Shasu Bedouin." [218][MAJIDBENAM_BR]The native Egyptian warrior class were the machimoi "who were, in origin, Libyans and posed a significant potential internal threat to royal authority." [219][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Mercenaries played an important role in the Egyptian navy. [220][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Greek mercenaries. Largest scale collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ Opet ♥ "The processions of images of the holy family of Thebes and of other sanctified beings (including statues of kings of olden times) setting forth from the huge brightly painted temples, and making their slow progress along formally arranged avenues with carefully stage-managed halts at intermediate way-stations, and the occasional excitement of a 'miracle': all this brought to the city as a whole spectacle and munificence which regularly reinforced the physical and economic dominance of the temples. And for the greatest of the festivals the king came to Thebes in person to be at the centre, and to absorb some of the power that the occasion generated. "This most important of festivals was the Festival of Opet. It came round every year, in the second month of the season of Inundation. In the mid-18th Dynasty it lasted for eleven days. By the end of the reign of Rameses III in the 20th it had been lengthened to no less than twenty-seven days. At that time at Medinet Habu the festival was celebrated by the distribution of 11,341 loaves, 85 cakes, and 385 jars of beer. The core of the festival was an unusually long procession of images of the holy family of Thebes. The route lay between Karnak itself and the temple of Luxor (Figure 97, p. 266). In the time of Hatsheput the outward journey was made by land, using the newly built southward extension of courts and pylons at Karnak, pausing at six way-stations, whilst the return journey was by river. By the late 18th Dynasty both the outward and the return journeys were being made by river. Each of the deities travelled in a separate barge, towed by smaller boats and by gangs of men on the bank, who could include high court officials, hauling on ropes. It was one of the occasions when the public could present pleas to the gods before their portable barques, and before colossal ka-statues of the king (see below for the meaning of ka [divine essence - EC]). Detailed scenes of the processions, depicting also the soldiers, dancers and musicians who followed the progress of the barges from the bank, were carved on the walls of Luxor temple, the destination of the festival. "[...] The procession of the Opet Festival took the king to the temple. Leaving the crowds outside he entered and proceeded in the company of priests to the enlcosed chambers at the back. There, in a charges incense-laden atmosphere and the mystic presence of the god Amun (and his ithyphallic manifestation Amun-Min), the king and his ka were merged, and the king's person transformed. When the king reappeared, he did so miraculously transformed into a divine being, 'Foremost of all the living Kas.' His reappearance in public freshly transfigured was the real climax, the moment of cheering which implied that the miracle had worked and had been accepted as having worked." [228] The following relates to festivals more generally: "Commoners could participate by witnessing a sacred performance, by communing with the gods through prayer and oracles, or simply by singing, dancing, and feasting. For ordinary Egyptians, festivals were a time of sensory stimulation through sound movements, scents, and the nervous anticipation of being in the company of the divine. Festivals were community affairs, a time for the residents of a village or town to abandon their daily tasks and come together in celebration. [...] Their repeated commemoration was part of the rhythm of life, providing security through predictability. This was particularly true of the celebrations marking the renewal of the king (such as the Opet or the Decade Festival), which symbolized the victory of order over chaos. [...] "Festivals also served to bolster state control and promote royal ideology. [...] The Opet and Sed (jubilee) festivals specifically commemorated the renewal of the king's power. [...]. "Festivals presented logistical and economic challenges. They required huge amounts of bread, beer, wine, and precious incense, and the preparation for the larger festivals involved massive mobilization of people and resources. If the king was to attend, the additional needs of the members of the royal court had to be met. This apparently meant that citizens could be subject to an unreasonable requisitioning of supplies. The Edict of Hormheb (Dynasty 18) expressly forbade the 'agents of the queen's estate' from harassing the 'local mayors, oppressing them and searching for the [supplies] for the trip downstream ... each year during the [festival of Opet].' According to the decree, 'The agents of the royal quarters would approach the mayors saying 'give the supplies which are lacking for the journey, for look, pharaoh is making the trip to the festival of Opet.'" [229]
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ yearly ♥ "It came round every year, in the second month of the season of Inundation." [230]
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ yearly ♥ "It came round every year, in the second month of the season of Inundation." [231]
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ not applicable ♥
♠ Duration ♣ 648 ♥ hours. 27 days. "By the end of the reign of Rameses III in the 20th it had been lengthened to no less than twenty-seven days." [232] Opet festival, annual event at Luxor from Queen Hatshepsut's reign, which initially lasted 11 days but had become extended to 27 days by the end of the New Kingdom.[233]
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ 11,000 ♥ Inferred from the following: "By the end of the reign of Rameses III in the 20th it had been lengthened to no less than twenty-seven days. At that time at Medinet Habu the festival was celebrated by the distribution of 11,341 loaves, 85 cakes, and 385 jars of beer." [234]
♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ elites ♥ Priesthood, ruling elites. "Female priestly roles were downgraded in the New Kingdom and the Third Intermediate Period. During those periods, women , were almost exclusively singers (shamyetor heste) in divine choirs that accompanied priests in processions around and through the temple; the higher ranks apparently followed the priests into the area near the sanctuary." [235] "How did one become a priest in ancient Egypt? [...] We know from autobiographical texts that some priests of the higher ranks were personally selected by the king. [...] Clearly, family background and social status were often taken into consideration. [...] Very often, a priest inherited his office from his father, or at least he was considered eligible because of the family's familiarity with the duties." [236]
♠ Inclusiveness (participants) ♣ whole polity ♥ At one stage or another, the following could be considered to be participants: the king, soldiers, commoners, priests, entertainers, high court officials, and, arguably, people whose labour was necessary for the festival, such as oarsmen and cooks [237].
♠ Inclusiveness (audience) ♣ not applicable ♥ "Commoners could participate by witnessing a sacred performance, by communing with the gods through prayer and oracles, or simply by singing, dancing, and feasting." [238] Judging from Kemp's description of this event [239], commoners witnessed this festival as a sacred performance, though they were involved more actively at other points. ♠ property/valuable items ♣ inferred present ♥ "Festivals presented logistical and economic challenges. They required huge amounts of bread, beer, wine, and precious incense, and the preparation for the larger festivals involved massive mobilization of people and resources. If the king was to attend, the additional needs of the members of the royal court had to be met. This apparently meant that citizens could be subject to an unreasonable requisitioning of supplies. The Edict of Hormheb (Dynasty 18) expressly forbade the 'agents of the queen's estate' from harassing the 'local mayors, oppressing them and searching for the [supplies] for the trip downstream ... each year during the [festival of Opet].' According to the decree, 'The agents of the royal quarters would approach the mayors saying 'give the supplies which are lacking for the journey, for look, pharaoh is making the trip to the festival of Opet.'" [275]

Most widespread collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ Khoiak ♥ "Osiris, the primary deity of the dead and the legendary first ruler of Egypt, was a god who symbolized eternal rebirth through the story of his own resurrection. His festival was celebrated in the fourth month of inundation, from the twelfth to the thirtieth day, when the Nile receded and crops began to sprout - a potent natural symbol of resurrection. The festival is known as Khoiak, after the name of the month in which it was celebrated. It is known from Middle Kingdom texts (the Ikhernofert stela and texts of King Neferhotep) and from a great number of private stelae and Middle Kingdom monuments from Abydos. Though we lack the overall 'script' for the festival, because the earlier records refer only to isolated events, later records, including Roman-era texts from the walls of the rooftop chapels of the Temple of Hathor at Dendera (Fi. 21), provide a fuller account of the Khoiak festival. "[...] "Unlike the localized Opet or Decade festivals, which were celebrated only in Thebes, the Khoiak Festival was celebrated throughout the country. The broad geographic spread of the festivities commemorated the dispersal of the parts of Osiris's body across all parts of Egypt. [...] "The festival had two major components, both of which provided opportunities for public participation. The first part of the festival was a dramatic reenactment of the death and resurrection of Osiris and the defeat of his foes. This was performed before the public, much like a Christian passion play. [...] The religious drama followed a set script, laid down on a papyrus scroll that kings could cite to prove the authenticity of the festivals they held. [...] The second aspect of the festival was the manufacture, procession, and ceremonial internment of Osiris figurines. New images of Osiris, his ennead (the eight gods associated with him), and the jackal god Wapwawet were made, and sacred boats, sledges, and shrines were constructed to convey the divine statues through the necropolis. "During the festival, the king was supposed to play the role of Horus, the faithful son who avenged the murder of Osiris. However, Middle Kingdom accounts indicate that the king appointed a surrogate to represent him during the festival, just as the First Priest of the god acted on behalf of the king in daily temple rituals (see Chapter 3). At Abydos, the priests claim to have played the role of deities, but we do not know whether they donned costumes or simply stood as representatives of the gods. Some parts of the ritual enactment took place in the temple to which the public had no access. Other parts took place where the gathered crowds could watch and participate. "The festival began with the arrival of the royal delegation. Within the dark confines of the Osiris Temple, a priest offered myrrh, wine, and other 'divine products' to 'Osiris, in all his identities.' The official Ikhernofert claimed that within the temple of the statue of Osiris was embellished with lapis, turquoise, gold, and 'all precious stones' and was clothed with garments. The statue was then placed in a new naos of gold, silver, copper, sesnedjem-wood, and cedar. Construction of the naos and of the god's boat was overseen by Ikernofert himself. "These preliminary acts complete, the procession set out from the temple and made itself visible to the waiting throngs. It set out through the necropolis, led by the statue of Wepwawet (especially appropriate, because his name means 'Opener of the Ways'), who acted as the protector of the gods. Dramatic tension was fueled by a reenactment of the attack on Osiris by the company of Seth, which retold the story of Osiris's murder on 'the sandbanks of Nedit.' [...] One can imagine the screams and groans from the assembled onlookers as the two factions clashed and Osiris was slain. As in passion plays, this sequence always concluded with the triumph of good, as the priest representing Horus defeated Seth's company. In his speech of victory, he proclaimed: 'I avenged Wennofer (Osiris) on that day of great fighting, and I felled all his enemies in the sandbanks of Nedit.' "The victory of Horus was followed by a 'great procession' simulating the sorrowful funeral of Osiris. [...] "Once at the tomb of Osiris, participants enacted the funeral rites and recited an invocation to Re - 'Come down to me!' - to bring the life-giving forces of the sun back to the dead god. Several references to dancing suggest that some of the ritual dance, perhaps to celebrate the victories of Horus and Thoth, was performed. Finally, the procession returned to the Osiris Temple, and the statue of the god was restored to its shrine. "The public participated in the festivals of Osiris through their observation of the procession presented during Khoiak but also in more personal and intimate ways, especially through the creation and manipulation of images of the mummiform god. [...] "Some of these figurines were made during the Khoiak Festival, kept for a year, and then ritually buried, whereas others became a part of an individual's funerary equipment." [281]

The festival is known as Khoiak, after the name of the month in which it was celebrated." [282]

Most frequent collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ daily offering ♥ "The daily offering ritual was performed in every functioning temple three times a day, in imitation of human meal times. [...] The offering ritual is known from the Old Kingdom, from the Pyramid Texts and the economic records of Neferirkare at Abu Sir, and it continued to be the essential temple ritual throughout Egyptian history. "The ritual consisted of the physical maintenance of the god, followed by the presentation of food and drink. All ritual actions were made in the name of the king, and it is he who is shown in the temple reliefs, although in reality a priest acted in his stead. Each morning as the sun rose, the temple staff would assemble for duty. The daily ritual involved a variety of ranks of priests - wabs, lector priests, and God's Fathers are specifically mentioned. The Chief Priest would be specially purified in a section of the temple called the per duat or 'the house of morning.' There, he washed his body with water, was purifed with incense, and rinsed his mouth with natron. Texts record, 'There was performed for him all ceremonies that are performed for the king,' transforming him from a priest into a proper surrogate for the king. He then joined the other priests, who had also undergone purification. They gathered the materials needed for the ritual and proceeded to the temple's sanctuary. Before entering, they began a series of recitations that accompanied their preparations - the utterance for lighting the fire; the utterances for taking the incense, putting the brazier on the censer, and putting incense on the fire; and, finally, the 'utterance for advancing to the holy place.' The sense that the ritual was in theory performed by the king is evidence in the Chief Priest's first ritual words: 'It is the king who sends me.' "Each step of the ritual was highly symbolic, referred to mythic events, and was accompanied by a set liturgy. To begin the ritual, the priest awakened the god who slept in the sealed shrine. Holding a candle, he entered the sanctuary, chanting, 'Awake in peace! May your awakening be peaceful!' He broke the seals of the door bolts of the shrine, and drew back the bolts - an action that was equated with removing the fingers of Seth from the eye of Horus. The priest then 'opened the sight of the god' as he swung open the doors of the shrine, and he 'kissed the ground,' prostrating himself before the god's shrine. Each ritual step was punctuated with a specific recitation and an offering of incense and adoration of the deity. In preparation for removing the god from his shrine, the priest scattered pure white sand on the floor of the sanctuary, symbolizing the mound of creation from which all life sprang. He then recited a liturgy for 'Laying Hands Upon the God,' and removed the statue from the shrine, placing it on the sand. After more purifications, the priest removed the deity's outer linen garments and jewelry and wiped away the unguents from the previous day's ritual. Once the statue was suitably cleansed, the priest offered the god lengths of red, white, and green cloth that symbolized the blood of Isis, the uraeus, and fertility. This was followed by the presentation of green and black eye cosmetics. The god was adorned with bracelets, a broad collar, and anklets, and given the appropriate scepters and headdress. The statue was then wrapped in his 'great garment', probably a linen wrapper such as those that were found with the divine statues in the tomb of Tutankhamun. "Now fully awakened and ready for the day, the god was presented with flowers, food, and drink. Economic texts specify that the food offerings consisted of grain, vegetables, wine, and cuts of meat and fowl. When the god was considered to be done, an interval that is not specified in the texts, the food was taken away and the god was readied for sleep. After further purifications, the god was returned to his shrine, the doors closed and seal. As a final ritual, the king performed the 'Bringing of the Foot,' in which he grasped a broomlike had-plant and backed out of the sanctuary, sweeping his footprints from the room. This procedure not only left the sanctuary in good order, but, it was believed, also prevented evil from approaching the god, presumably by following the footprints of the priest-king. "A version of this ritual was repeated at midday and in the evening. The evidence for how elaborate these other two daily rituals were is inconclusive, but it is clear that the morning service was the most complete. The evening service may have been essentially food offerings, but the shrine was not opened, and the activity took place in the antechamber rather than directly before the god." [319]

♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ elites ♥ "Texts indicated that the priests who assisted in the daily offering service were drawn from the ranks of wabs, lectors, and God's Fathers, all of whom, in some time periods, were part-time priests (see Chapter 2). They served 'in their time,' that is, for a month, and then reverted to their other occupation(s). Because these ranks of priests were draw from the community, many men had firsthand experience with temple ritual." [323] "Female priestly roles were downgraded in the New Kingdom and the Third Intermediate Period. During those periods, women , were almost exclusively singers (shamyetor heste) in divine choirs that accompanied priests in processions around and through the temple; the higher ranks apparently followed the priests into the area near the sanctuary." [324]

♠ Orthopraxy checks ♣ present ♥ The Chief Priest's supervision [350]. HH: Temples constituted of a library, house of books, and a "house of life" in these two rooms extensive lists of rituals and hymns. These rooms acted like a ritual "repertoire" which ensured temple rituals were kept and functioned permanently. This is of course in addition to temple reliefs which projects how the rituals would have taken place and the various rules surrounding them (Shafer, Byron 1997 Temples, priests and rituals. In Temples of ancient Egypt, ed. Byron Shafer, pp.22-23. Ithaca: Cornell University Press). (KB: Added evidence copied from daily offering in EgOldK1)
♠ Orthodoxy checks ♣ present ♥ The Chief Priest's supervision of the ritual and his utterances. For example, "The sense that the ritual was in theory performed by the king is evidence in the Chief Priest's first ritual words: 'It is the king who sends me.'" [351] Moreover, "Scenes of the ritual s enacted in the sanctuary were also used to decorate the interior walls of the temples. [...] This area of the temple was accessible to ordinary people, as indicated by inscriptions on the architraves that say that it was a place of popular assembly where people could adore the king and the gods." ♠ human sacrifice of an out-group member ♣ absent ♥ Relatively detailed contemporary descriptions do not mention human sacrifice, as suggested by Teeter's summary of the ritual [356].
♠ human sacrifice of an in-group member ♣ absent ♥ Relatively detailed contemporary descriptions do not mention human sacrifice, as suggested by Teeter's summary of the ritual [357].

Most euphoric collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ Feast of the Valley ♥ "The 'Beautiful Feast of the Valley' was celebrated only in Thebes (Map 2). [...] "The Feast of the Valley was observed once a year, on the first day of the second month of summer in conjunction with the appearance of the new moon. It lasted for two days. The first record of the feast dates to Dynasty 11. The festival was most popular in the New Kingdom, the Ramesside Period, and Dynasties 25-26, but there are references to the festival as late as 117 BC during the reign of Ptolemy VIII. By that time, however, the festival had likely changed character, taking on more Osirian overtones. [...] "The focus of the festival was the parade of statues portraying the gods and deceased royalty (Plate VIII). [...] The procession, led by the king, was accompanied by throngs of priests who carried the great fans signifying the presence of the deity and by huge crowds of locals. [...] "Graffiti note that priests watched for the procession from the heights of the west bank. It must have been an exciting and prestigious duty, for some of the priests recorded that they stood lookout for several years in a row. One the flotilla was spotted, mobs of people who lived on the west bank joined the crowd that had followed the procession from the east. Everyone was dressed in his or her finest white linen clothing, clutching flowers and food offerings as they greeted the 'divine arrival' as it moored on the west bank. "[...] People crowded around the pageant clamoring for a view of the gods. One can imagine the roadways and canals of the west bank lined with people laden with bouquets of flowers and baskets of offerings, and crying out in honor of the gods. "[...] "During the first day of the festival, the procession wound its way through the necropolis. The musicians and onlookers made - intentionally - a lot of noise, for according to the texts, those buried in the western necropolis harkened to the sound of the procession. The clamor enlivened them and caused them to emerge from their tombs to see Amun and to meet with their families. "During the festival, priests could participate by carrying the divine boat or walking alongside it, holding the broad fans that announced the divine presence. A much larger number of ordinary people took part as well, by observing the procession and enacting rituals at the family tombs. For them, the most important aspect of the Feast of the Valley was the sensual communion between the living and the dead that took place at the tombs. "[...] The properties of smell, sound, and taste were thought to be capable of transcending the barrier between life and death to reach the deceased and bring him or her into the celebration. These senses were further stimulated by copious amounts of beer and wine, which created an ecstatic state and brought the living closer to he dead. The revelers called on Hathor, addressing her as the 'Lady of Drunkenness.' The families encouraged each other: 'For your ka! Drink the intoxicating drink! Celebrate a beautiful day ... may your heart be refreshed in your house [i.e., tomb].' "The necropolis was filled with activity, noise, smells, and music. Fueled by alcohol and the excitement of the festival, the living recited hymns to their ancestors' statues to encourage the spirits of the deceased to enliven them; 'Emerge from the earth! Behold Ra and follow Amun in his beautiful Feast of the Valley!' The spirits were encouraged to join the procession: 'May you be in the crew of the royal boat and may you hear the clamor in the temples in wester Thebes. May you see Amun in the Beautiful Feast of the Valley and follow him to the temple precincts.' "Bands of musicians circulated through the necropolis visiting individual tombs. Women shook beaded necklaces (menat) and clanged their metallic rattles (sistra) (see Plate III) both instruments sacred to Hathor, while male musicians clapped and sang, creating a hypnotic rhythm that reverberated among the tombs. Their refrains continued into the night[...]" "The odor of food filled the necropolis. The dead were presented with fragrant roasted birds and meat. Sweet myrrh oil was poured on the meats, making the scents even more alluring. [...] "Another source of sensory stimulation was the enormous, fragrant bouquet of flowers, called an ankh (a pun on the word ankh that also means 'life'), that each family presented to its deceased ancestors (Plate X). [...] "All these rituals were enacted to produce an ecstatic union of the dead and the living - to bring the living into the realm of the dead and the dead back to the living." [359]
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ yearly ♥ "The Feast of the Valley was observed once a year, on the first day of the second month of summer in conjunction with the appearance of the new moon." [360]
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ yearly ♥ "The Feast of the Valley was observed once a year, on the first day of the second month of summer in conjunction with the appearance of the new moon." [361]
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ not applicable ♥ "The Feast of the Valley was observed once a year, on the first day of the second month of summer in conjunction with the appearance of the new moon." [362]
♠ Duration ♣ 48 ♥ hours. "It lasted for two days." [363]
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ suspected unknown ♥ in absolute numbers
♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ elites ♥ ruling elites. "Female priestly roles were downgraded in the New Kingdom and the Third Intermediate Period. During those periods, women , were almost exclusively singers (shamyetor heste) in divine choirs that accompanied priests in processions around and through the temple; the higher ranks apparently followed the priests into the area near the sanctuary." [364] "How did one become a priest in ancient Egypt? [...] We know from autobiographical texts that some priests of the higher ranks were personally selected by the king. [...] Clearly, family background and social status were often taken into consideration. [...] Very often, a priest inherited his office from his father, or at least he was considered eligible because of the family's familiarity with the duties." [365]
♠ Inclusiveness (participants) ♣ whole polity ♥ Commoners observed the procession but participated directly in the more euphoric aspects of this festival. "A much larger number of ordinary people took part as well, by observing the procession and enacting rituals at the family tombs. For them, the most important aspect of the Feast of the Valley was the sensual communion between the living and the dead that took place at the tombs." [366]
♠ Inclusiveness (audience) ♣ not applicable ♥ Commoners observed the procession but participated directly in the more euphoric aspects of this festival. ♠ property/valuable items ♣ inferred present ♥ "Festivals presented logistical and economic challenges. They required huge amounts of bread, beer, wine, and precious incense, and the preparation for the larger festivals involved massive mobilization of people and resources. If the king was to attend, the additional needs of the members of the royal court had to be met. This apparently meant that citizens could be subject to an unreasonable requisitioning of supplies." [394]

Phase II Variables (polity-based)[edit]

Limits on Power of the Chief Executive[edit]

♠ Executive power is separate or independent from military ♣ absent ♥ The king had close advisors in a High Council. Those who acquired the title "general" were not necessarily of the military.[400] In fact, the chief physician Udjahorresnet was also a leader of forces, and an admiral of a fleet,[401] so titles of high officials did not imply specialism. Before the Saite Period: "During almost three centuries, the priests of Amun had become independent of the royal power."[402] The Saites had to deal with two aristocracies, Libyans in the Delta and the Thebans in Upper Egypt which were also linked by marriage.[403] The Saites used foreign mercenaries which essentially centralized control.
♠ Executive power is separate or independent from legislative ♣ absent ♥ The king had close advisors in a High Council. Those who acquired the title "general" were not necessarily of the military.[404] In fact, the chief physician Udjahorresnet was also a leader of forces, and an admiral of a fleet,[405] so titles of high officials did not imply specialism.
♠ Executive power is separate or independent from judiciary ♣ absent ♥ Manager of the Antechamber organized royal audiences. In the Petition of Peteise (P. Rylands 9) the Manager of the Antechamber's had a scribe-accountant who informed him of a case of extortion in the regions. The Manager of the Antechamber then wrote to the governor and the general of the nome in question telling them to make arrests. "This shows that the Manager of the Antechamber had genuine powers of investigation and command that allowed him to resolve personally some of the matters brought to the attention of the sovereign. For this purpose he had his own investigation service; thus he was not a mere 'chamberlain' in charge of maintaining the list of hearings and introducing petitioners."[406] The executive therefore could be both the investigator and the judge, although courts probably were held in temples.[MAJIDBENAM_BR] ♠ Right to petition executive ♣ inferred present ♥ "The Petition of P3-di-3st (C.I.17) offers valuable information for reconstructing the administrative structure and social background of the Saite Dynasty and the early Persian Egypt".[407] P3-di-3st was a shipmaster. Scholarly opinion on the document ranges from it having a literary character to being a real legal document, or a draft for one.[408]
♠ Critique of executive by government ♣ present ♥ King had advisers. The statuette of General Djedptahiufankh says the king "relies on his words on the day of the High Council ... distinguished by the king because of his excellent ideas ... pronouncing wise judgements in the Council of Nobles ... and speaking to them next to the king so that they were satisfied by his remarks."[409]
♠ Critique of executive by non-government ♣ inferred present ♥ "Both documents ... contain texts dealing with oracles, and the editor has taken the opportunity of including in his publication an important essay on Egyptian oracles, written by Professor Cerny, and based on a lecture given in Chicago in 1955. Oracles of all kinds are discussed - public and private, religious and judicial. High affairs of State might be settled by oracular pronouncement; humble inquiries of simple men might equally be answered..."[410] Also Libyans in Council of Nobles in first Saite period?[MAJIDBENAM_BR] ♠ Sanction of executive by government ♣ inferred absent ♥
♠ Sanction of executive by non-government ♣ inferred absent ♥
♠ Constraint on executive by government ♣ present ♥ High Council and Council of Nobles. King had advisers. The statuette of General Djedptahiufankh says the king "relies on his words on the day of the High Council ... distinguished by the king because of his excellent ideas ... pronouncing wise judgements in the Council of Nobles ... and speaking to them next to the king so that they were satisfied by his remarks."[411]
♠ Constraint on executive by non-government ♣ inferred present ♥ "Both documents ... contain texts dealing with oracles, and the editor has taken the opportunity of including in his publication an important essay on Egyptian oracles, written by Professor Cerny, and based on a lecture given in Chicago in 1955. Oracles of all kinds are discussed - public and private, religious and judicial. High affairs of State might be settled by oracular pronouncement; humble inquiries of simple men might equally be answered..."[412]
♠ Impeachment ♣ inferred absent ♥ ♠ Informal critique of executive ♣ inferred present ♥ "Both documents ... contain texts dealing with oracles, and the editor has taken the opportunity of including in his publication an important essay on Egyptian oracles, written by Professor Cerny, and based on a lecture given in Chicago in 1955. Oracles of all kinds are discussed - public and private, religious and judicial. High affairs of State might be settled by oracular pronouncement; humble inquiries of simple men might equally be answered..."[413] Council of Nobles intended for Libyan aristocracy? "Only the High Chiefs who rejected this type of compromise would have been ultimately defeated. Once integrated into the administration, the descendants of the High Chiefs would have been gradually 'digested' by the Saite power. ... In this perspective, the Council of Nobles may have characterized a very particular period, that of the transition of a royal power based on the consent of the High Chiefs to a more absolute form of monarchy."[414]
♠ Informal sanction of executive ♣ inferred absent ♥
♠ Informal constraint on executive ♣ present ♥ "the support of prominent families of the kingdom was crucial for the stability of the monarchy."[415] general reference "For about half of the Late Period the country kept its independence, but that was often due to a reliance on foreign mercenaries who had adopted the new [military] methods."[416] In the Late Period "almost all officials had priesthoods."[417] "The Saite monarchy controlled the Egyptian territory thanks to the submission of two aristocracies. The first of them was the Libyan aristocracy, in which the Saites originated and whose representatives held the major cities of the Delta. The second was organized around the temple of Amun of Thebes and the major local priestly families that supported the Kushite dynasty."[418] Council of Nobles intended for Libyan aristocracy? "Only the High Chiefs who rejected this type of compromise would have been ultimately defeated. Once integrated into the administration, the descendants of the High Chiefs would have been gradually 'digested' by the Saite power. ... In this perspective, the Council of Nobles may have characterized a very particular period, that of the transition of a royal power based on the consent of the High Chiefs to a more absolute form of monarchy."[419]
♠ Informal impeachment ♣ inferred absent ♥ ♠ Critique of executive by religious agents ♣ inferred present ♥ The priestly office of God's Wife of Amun had "very great and publicly acknowledged" political importance.[420] In the Late Period "almost all officials had priesthoods."[421]
♠ Sanction of executive by religious agents ♣ inferred absent ♥ The priestly office of God's Wife of Amun had "very great and publicly acknowledged" political importance.[422] - but not able to sanction the king.
♠ Constraint on executive by religious agents ♣ inferred present ♥ The priestly office of God's Wife of Amun had "very great and publicly acknowledged" political importance.[423] In the Late Period "almost all officials had priesthoods."[424] "Oracles were used in the election of certain officials, or even kings."[425]
♠ Constraint on executive by religious institutions ♣ inferred present ♥ The priestly office of God's Wife of Amun had "very great and publicly acknowledged" political importance.[426] In the Late Period "almost all officials had priesthoods."[427] "Oracles were used in the election of certain officials, or even kings."[428] To get Upper Egypt under control Saites used an observor (rsw, in Thebes), often also given the title of "governor", called Nesnaisut. "It was very certainly in the same period that the position of God's Wife of Amun, held by the Kushite royal princesses, came under the control of the Saites."[429]
♠ Impeachment by religious agents ♣ inferred absent ♥ "Oracles were used in the election of certain officials, or even kings."[430] no mention of impeachment
♠ Subordination of the chief executive to a religious agent ♣ inferred absent ♥ The priestly office of God's Wife of Amun had "very great and publicly acknowledged" political importance. "Like a king they used multiple throne names and wrote them in cartouches." There also were similarities in burial arrangement.[431] However, since the God's Wife was chosen by the Pharoah this religious agent was at least in theory subordinate to the chief executive. "Oracles were used in the election of certain officials, or even kings."[432]
♠ Motivation by belief in supernatural agent ♣ present ♥ "By the Late Period, the civil administration of the country seems to have been conducted by people whose temple statues - their principal monuments - displayed titles that were almost exclusively religious."[433] "In their conduct Egyptians felt that they were guided, instructed and inspired by God" through the oracles."[434][MAJIDBENAM_BR] ♠ Customary mechanism ♣ present ♥ Generally hereditary monarchy. General Amasis who himself "interrupted the dynastic continuity" was succeeded by his son.[435]
♠ Legal mechanism ♣ inferred present ♥ "Oracles were used in the election of certain officials, or even kings."[436]
♠ Number of power transitions ♣ 6 ♥ 26th Dynasty 0. Nekau I (672-664 BCE) 1. Psamtek I (Wahibra) (664-610 BCE) 2. Nekau II (Wehemibra) (610-595 BCE) 3. Psamtek II (Neferibra) (595-589 BCE) 4. Apries (Haaibra) (589-570 BCE) 5. Ahmose II [Amasis] (Khnemibra) (570-526 BCE) 6. Psamtek III (Ankhkaenra) (526-525 BCE) - Cambyses (525-522 BCE)[437]
♠ Number of contested transitions ♣ 3 ♥ General Amasis overthrew Apries.[438] Cambyses overthrew General Amasis's son, Psamtik III.[439] "The chief physician of the king, Harsiesis son of Ramose, was reported to have been involved in a plot aimed at assassination of a Saite sovereign."[440]
♠ Number of successful overturns ♣ 2 ♥ General Amasis overthrew Apries.[441] Cambyses overthrew General Amasis's son, Psamtik III.[442]

Central Bureaucracy[edit]

♠ State-level administrators ♣ present ♥ "the second part of the Saite period witnesses the development of a financial administration. The Manager of the scribes of the council manages the audit office of the Royal Household. The Manager of the royal boats handles logistics liaisons among various parts of the royal domain. An inspection office composed of Managers of the fields is in charge of protecting the royal lands and their products from attempts at seizure. The senti, top-level administrator in charge of the sacred domains, appears at the very end of the period."[443] "specialized departments composed of financial, tax, logistics, and other specialists assigned to monitor local administrations" from about 591 BCE.[444][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"By the Late Period, the civil administration of the country seems to have been conducted by people whose temple statues - their principal monuments - displayed titles that were almost exclusively religious."[445] In the Late Period there was a "central state".[446][MAJIDBENAM_BR]In the Late Period the title "overseer of the treasuries of the hnw" confirms a "physical link of the pr-nswt to an administrative institution like the treasury, as already observed in narrative inscriptions."[447]
♠ Prestige of state-level bureaucracy ♣ equal: 664-591 BCE; higher: 592-525 BCE ♥"the second part of the Saite period witnesses the development of a financial administration. The Manager of the scribes of the council manages the audit office of the Royal Household. The Manager of the royal boats handles logistics liaisons among various parts of the royal domain. An inspection office composed of Managers of the fields is in charge of protecting the royal lands and their products from attempts at seizure. The senti, top-level administrator in charge of the sacred domains, appears at the very end of the period."[448] "specialized departments composed of financial, tax, logistics, and other specialists assigned to monitor local administrations" from about 591 BCE.[449][MAJIDBENAM_BR]According to Quirke the k3p and the hnty "might be identified respectively with 'the Inner palace' and 'the Outer palace'."[450][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Authority over state-level administrators
♠ Direct ♣ present ♥ A "superintendent of the central treasury" and a "chief steward" are known under Apries and Amasis.[451] From about 591 BCE: "specialized departments composed of financial, tax, logistics, and other specialists assigned to monitor local administrations".[452]
♠ Indirect ♣ inferred absent ♥ From about 591 BCE: "specialized departments composed of financial, tax, logistics, and other specialists assigned to monitor local administrations".[453]
♠ Diffused ♣ inferred absent: 664-592 BCE; absent: 591-525 BCE ♥ From about 591 BCE: "specialized departments composed of financial, tax, logistics, and other specialists assigned to monitor local administrations".[454]
♠ Mixed ♣ inferred absent ♥ previous code: inferred present (unclear what used to support this claim; evidence cited has nothing about mixed authority, and only Direct is coded present above) | "Oracles were used in the election of certain officials"[455] From about 591 BCE: "specialized departments composed of financial, tax, logistics, and other specialists assigned to monitor local administrations".[456]
♠ Governmental control ♣ ♥ The priestly office of God's Wife of Amun came to be held by a princess rather than a queen, who "had to remain celibate and adopted her successor. The king decided who that woman should be."[457]
♠ Non-governmental oversight ♣ ♥ "By the Late Period, the civil administration of the country seems to have been conducted by people whose temple statues - their principal monuments - displayed titles that were almost exclusively religious."[458] "Oracles were used in the election of certain officials"[459][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Source of Support of state-level officials

♠ Corruption ♣ widespread ♥ Conflicts around temples during reign of Amasis. "A memorandum from Thebes, for instance, records that raising taxes from the temples in the area of Elephantine was subject to the interference of 'enemies', while a contemporary governor was said to have usurped some income from the temple of Khentamenti in Abydos.[462]
♠ Bribery ♣ ♥
♠ Abuse of power ♣ ♥ Power, authority and influence in Ancient Egyptian bureaucracy also "exerted through networks of social and personal relations (from marriages to favorites, from reliability to co-optation), through the use of informal networks of power (like patronage or local potentates), and also through the mobilization of immaterial resources like prestige, charisma, and religion."[463]
♠ Professionalization of state-level bureaucracy ♣ inferred present ♥ A "superintendent of the central treasury" and a "chief steward" are known under Apries and Amasis.[464] "the second part of the Saite period witnesses the development of a financial administration. The Manager of the scribes of the council manages the audit office of the Royal Household. The Manager of the royal boats handles logistics liaisons among various parts of the royal domain. An inspection office composed of Managers of the fields is in charge of protecting the royal lands and their products from attempts at seizure. The senti, top-level administrator in charge of the sacred domains, appears at the very end of the period."[465] "specialized departments composed of financial, tax, logistics, and other specialists assigned to monitor local administrations" from about 591 BCE.[466]

Local-level officials (provincial, regional, civic administration)[edit]

♠ Number of regional administrative units ♣ 2: 664-592 BCE ♥ ?: 591-525 BCE. Initially Psamtik I seemed to ignore the nome division and merged Middle and Upper Egypt into an administrative region called 'the Southern Land' a practice similar to the later Persian use of 'Southern Marches' for Upper Egypt.[469][MAJIDBENAM_BR]By 591 BCE "There seems to have been a return to a more traditional division in nomes ruled from a capital city under the authority of a governor."[470] Middle Egypt now under greater control from this time.[471][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Governorate palaces may have been built.[472]

♠ Direct ♣ inferred absent: 664-592 BCE; inferred present: 591-525 BCE ♥ In the Late Period "People traveled less about the country for reasons of state, and the central state intervened less in local affairs."[477] By 591 BCE "There seems to have been a return to a more traditional division in nomes ruled from a capital city under the authority of a governor."[478] From 591 BCE Middle Egypt now under greater control" due to return to nomes administration.[479] "In sum, the first Saite Period, the seventh century, was a reign of skillful politics aimed at taking over the territory. The second period, the sixth century (the years 592-591 could well mark this turning point), was an age of administrative standardization, and P. Rylands 9 reveals a country divided into nomes (ts.w)" e.g. Oxyrhynchos, Hermopolis, Cynopolis. "even if the territorial powers of intermediate level between the nome and the city, like the 'district-q'h.t', continue to be problematic."[480]
♠ Indirect ♣ inferred absent: 664-592 BCE; inferred present: 591-525 BCE ♥ Saite hold on Upper Egypt increased over time. "the campaign conducted by Psamtik II against the kingdom of Napata in 592-591 ... Before that date, the Saites had recognized the Kushite sovereigns of the Twenty-fifth Dynasty as legitimate. Thereafter, however, the Nubian kings were considered - retrospectively - to be usurpers. The cartouches of Piye and his successors, and those of the Divide Adoratrice connected with them, were therefore systematically smashed. The eradication of the Kushite past directly affected the aristocracy in the Theban region, which had prospered under the Twenty-fith Dynasty."[481] At Busiris in the Delta there was a Saite administrator, described as a "count and governor".[482] New Kingdom text "The Duties of the Vizier" (TT 100) "refer to the pr-nswt as the centre of royal government where two important dignities of the administration, the vizier and the treasurer, performed their functions" and received "reports from Egyptian provinces to update the government on happenings in outlying areas of the state."[483][484] "the demonstration of force in 592-591 had put an end to a period of compromise, the Theban region had been integrated into the Saite kingdom."[485]
♠ Diffused ♣ inferred present ♥ To unify the Delta region Psamtik I had to "impose his power, in an area in which political unity had collapsed, to the benefit of principalities held by 'warlods' of Libyan origin ... Different methods appear to have been used for the treatment of the competing lines."[486] Sebennytos was controlled by a Libyan family.[487] Busiris was controlled by a Saite "count and governor".[488] However, "the highest representative of the principality of Sebennytos ultimately recognized the authority of Psamtik I. In this case, the new power and the former Libyan chiefs had thus found a compromise that allowed the members of the old-line families of the High Chiefs of the Delta who had so decided to rally to and join the local Saite administration."[489] To get Upper Egypt under control Saites used an observor (rsw, in Thebes), often also given the title of "governor", called Nesnaisut. "It was very certainly in the same period that the position of God's Wife of Amun, held by the Kushite royal princesses, came under the control of the Saites."[490]
♠ Mixed ♣ present ♥ To unify the Delta region Psamtik I had to "impose his power, in an area in which political unity had collapsed, to the benefit of principalities held by 'warlods' of Libyan origin ... ♠ Corruption ♣ significant ♥ P. Rylands 9 (16,1-18) "shows a royal administrator holding the title of Manager of the fields (mr 3h) arriving at Teudjoi and finding two property-management irregularities. ... To punish the offenders, the royal administrator consfiscated the statue domain, the island, and 4,000 sacks of grain harvested from the lands held illegally. In his speech ... pronounced before the king himself, the Manager of the fields appears as the guardian of property integrity in charge of ensuring that land belonging to the royal domain was not improperly annexed by a temple (as in the case of the island), and that the products of such lands were not diverted by dishonest individuals."[497] After c591 BCE the governor did not have police authority to make arrests. A central official had to ask the local official with the title of 'general.'[498] "The governor thus could not issue orders to the general; in other words, he did not have the power to command troops of soldiers or police agents stationed in the nome."[499] This might suggest that abuse of power was more common in first than the second Saite Period, that the change was an attempt to fix the problem of local governors not doing what they were told or abusing their powers to make arrests. However, conflicts around temples during reign of Amasis suggests corruption still significant in later period? This tax would have been similar to the Athenian eisphora, a contribution based on estimate of wealth (timema) and imposed by the Athenians starting in 428, with this difference: in the case of Egypt, it would have been applied to several million taxpayers."[505]

[507][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"The papyrus deals with the request by ... to the Prophet of Amun, ..., that he should try to get payment for their dung from the two sons of ... It appears that they had paid a quantity of grain, perhaps for taxes, to the granary. This suggests that it is harvest time and .... perceives that it is a good time to obtain payment while the two have the resources to pay for it. ... In the Petition of Peteise (P. Rylands 9) the Manager of the Antechamber's had a scribe-accountant who informed him of a case of extortion in the regions. The Manager of the Antechamber then wrote to the governor and the general of the nome in question telling them to make arrests. "This shows that the Manager of the Antechamber had genuine powers of investigation and command that allowed him to resolve personally some of the matters brought to the attention of the sovereign. For this purpose he had his own investigation service; thus he was not a mere 'chamberlain' in charge of maintaining the list of hearings and introducing petitioners."[509]

[527][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"Both documents ... contain texts dealing with oracles, and the editor has taken the opportunity of including in his publication an important essay on Egyptian oracles, written by Professor Cerny, and based on a lecture given in Chicago in 1955. Oracles of all kinds are discussed - public and private, religious and judicial. High affairs of State might be settled by oracular pronouncement; humble inquiries of simple men might equally be answered..."[528][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Oracles had an increased role in the administration of justice in the Late Period.[529] "The use of oracles remained a feature of Egyptian religion through the Roman period."[530]

Property Rights[edit]

♠ Private property (land) ♣ present ♥ Two basic types of property in Egypt: “associated with the holding of an office, and personal property.” [531]
♠ Usufruct ♣ present ♥ “Land-lease contracts between private landowners and tenants are also known during the Kushite (c. 760-656 BCE) and Saite (c. 685-525 BCE) dynasties. Many of the parties to lease contracts had temple-status designations, but whether they obtained their fields by virtue of that status is unknown.” “Even if some priests had did receive temple land for their service, they were still able to sell that land to someone else and to have their children inherit it.” [532]
♠ Exclusivity ♣ inferred present ♥ “On some occasions, a sale of temple land to an individual is recorded. Such transactions imply at least a more permanent transfer of temple land to a private party.” [533]
♠ Partibility ♣ present ♥ “The list of real property is also noteworthy. Most of it was held jointly, and much of the property consisted of buildings or underdeveloped plots.” [534] “Land-lease contracts between private landowners and tenants are also known during the Kushite (c. 760-656 BCE) and Saite (c. 685-525 BCE) dynasties. Many of the parties to lease contracts had temple-status designations, but whether they obtained their fields by virtue of that status is unknown.” “Even if some priests had did receive temple land for their service, they were still able to sell that land to someone else and to have their children inherit it.” [535]
♠ Lease ♣ present ♥ "leasing of lands belonging to a sacred domain"[536] “As in earlier periods, priests retained a personal claim to temple land…although we are left in the dark as to how the land was acquired….despite what may have been a standard practice of giving priests claim to land that they could either farm themselves or lease out for income...” [537] “Land-lease contracts between private landowners and tenants are also known during the Kushite (c. 760-656 BCE) and Saite (c. 685-525 BCE) dynasties. Many of the parties to lease contracts had temple-status designations, but whether they obtained their fields by virtue of that status is unknown.” “Even if some priests had did receive temple land for their service, they were still able to sell that land to someone else and to have their children inherit it.” [538] "we can suppose that the land entrusted to a member of the clergy was part of his remuneration, and that he was to cultivate it directly or lease it to a tenant."[539]
♠ Lease Rights ♣ inferred present ♥ “The elites, while exploitative, were conscious of the necessity of providing certain basic services and recognizing particular rights in order to ensure social stability.” [540]
♠ Transfer of property ♣ present ♥ “On some occasions, a sale of temple land to an individual is recorded. Such transactions imply at least a more permanent transfer of temple land to a private party.” [541] “Land-lease contracts between private landowners and tenants are also known during the Kushite (c. 760-656 BCE) and Saite (c. 685-525 BCE) dynasties. ♠ Use of property ♣ inferred absent ♥ Not mentioned in sources.
♠ Property market ♣ inferred present ♥ “The documents stressed that a ‘satisfactory price’ had been received by the vendor and that he/she will guarantee exclusive ownership of the property being conveyed, and that no action will be brought in respect to the property.” [543] Property sales not registered and taxed until the Ptolemies. “Private conveyances of arable land by sale and donation are attested from the tenth century BCE onwards.” “The only known limit on the owner’s rights to such property was the fiscal burden that the king and the temple’s imposed on land within their domains” [544]
♠ Other property ownership ♣ present ♥ “How much land there was in private hands at the time of the Ptolemaic takeover we cannot say in exact terms, but much of it was probably small-scale holding within family groups with, of course institutional claims attached. The terms ‘ownership’ and ‘possession’ have caused much institutional debate when it comes to the interpretation of Egyptian evidence. Indeed the confusion stems from the vagueness of the ancient terminology- the demotic term for ‘ownership’ and ‘possession’ is the same. From the point of view of the holder, there was of course, little distinction. Ownership is an abstraction, implying the enforcement of rights by the state.” [545][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Inheritance System
♠ partible inheritance ♣ present ♥ Traditional Egyptian form of inheritance. [546]
♠ patrilineal inheritance ♣ present ♥ “As in the Egyptian inheritance law of the New Kingdom, the Hermopolis Legal Code, allowed the oldest son or oldest daughter (if there were no sons) to act as the principal heir and trustee for undivided property of all the heirs, including land.” Used in a trial in 170 BCE. [547]
♠ matrilineal inheritance ♣ ♥ “As in the Egyptian inheritance law of the New Kingdom, the Hermopolis Legal Code, allowed the oldest son or oldest daughter (if there were no sons) to act as the principal heir and trustee for undivided property of all the heirs, including land.” Used in a trial in 170 BCE. [548]
♠ primogeniture inheritance by only males ♣ present ♥ “As in the Egyptian inheritance law of the New Kingdom, the Hermopolis Legal Code, allowed the oldest son or oldest daughter (if there were no sons) to act as the principal heir and trustee for undivided property of all the heirs, including land.” Used in a trial in 170 BCE. [549]

♠ Property can be owned by both males and females ♣ present ♥ Women could own and manage property in Late Period Egypt.[563] "From the abundance of relevant data available to date it emanates that many varations of property rights could pass through women's hands. In this respect we hear of real estate (fields, grounds and houses), of serfs and cattle, of money and other movables, also of benefices and emoluments arising from chapels, tombs and offices. ... The texts give to understand that women's possessions were indeed their own; the basis of their economic position was primarily their right to share in the property of their families (through dowry, donation, inheritance conventions, etc)."[564][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Discrimination on the basis of social class (include de facto limitations even when there are no explicit legal restrictions)

♠ Property can be owned by people from all social classes ♣ inferred present ♥ Choachytes were funerary workers on tombs of deceased individuals paid from a land donation made to the temple by the deceased. The choachytes represented "a middle class that profited from the shortage of insitutional workers to take charge of land while at the same time developing small businesses ... The phenomenon fed itself. Small entrepreneurs like Djekhy and his son simultaneously operated a mortuary service business and leased fields for the cultivation of flax for the textile market. ... simplified cursive writing styles ... constituted an indispensable tool for formalizing a multitude of minor business transactions involving both agriculture and services."[566] "A royal tax was not assessed against tenants of the temple lands until the Ptolemaic era."[567][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Discrimination on the basis of ethnicity/race
